In article <500103F1dtdh9U1@mid.individual.net>,
Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
>
>"Michael Mattias" <mmattias@talsystems.com> wrote in message 
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>An online transaction does that, where values of A and B = 'now'.

When I hear something like this in a meeting my first response is to walk 
out... and I usually manage to squelch it.

My second response is to laugh and say 'Oh... *someone's* been getting 
their system-design from Airline Magazines again, haven't they?'... and I 
usually manage to squelch *that*, too.

My third response is a furrowed brow, a smile and then 'Of course... 
Newton came up with a similar concept for his foundation of the calculus, 
in the Principia Mathamematica, when he dealt with the prime and ultimate 
ratios to deal with nascent and evanascent quantities.  You do realise, of 
course, that this completely and utterly neglects such difficulties as a 
head's search speeds and limitations imposed by the data path width of a 
system, correct?'...

... and I *don't* usually manage to squelch that, and it usually leads to 
the bloviator turning read and saying 'No, you Just Don't Understand...' 
... and then I don't get invited to other meetings and that suits me just 
fine, thankee.

>
>As history is simply a collection of 'now' moments, batch processing can be 
>obviated by extending the scope of the transaction to do accrual. 

Batch processing cannot be obviated (in the sense of 'to make 
unnecessary') as long as there are Corner Office Idiots who demand that 
reports be on their desks (or, within the past few years, viewable on 
their terminals) by a certain time on a certain date... and such Corner 
Office Idiots often have behind them - in the case of, say, tax reporting 
or Corporate Position Statements - the Force of Law.  I've never tried 
telling a State Auditor 'Oh, the quarterlies were obviated by considering 
the intervening time to be a series of Eternal Nows'... I'd be interested 
in hearing your experiences in having done so.

DD
```

all time is relative, and to a human observer, the time a disk head takes to

No doubt you would... :-)

Triggered transactions and stored procedures,  in conjunction with a 
transactional approach to system design, can, theoretically, produce the 
same results as batch processing, within the same time constraints. Batch 
processing is not the ONLY way to get time sensitive reports and legally 
required documents produced  within the constraints required by your COI.

Having said that, it is as good a way as any, in terms of what is currently 
viable. My position in this discussion is that such may not ALWAYS be the 
case.

Perhaps if you read the whole thread...?

Or then again...perhaps not... :-)

Pete.

Just a point here - what may be causing confusion is that batch processing 
is every bit as transactional as on-line near-real-time processing. In batch 
processing, all the transactions are just batched together. ;)

The point is I don't think we are talking about the difference between 
'batch' and 'transactional' processing so much as between 'batch' and 
'on-line' transactions.

In the first case, I think there is little or no meaning, while in the 
second is something that you might have a really good theory you are 
developing.

-Paul

>> Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

<snip>
>
> Triggered transactions and stored procedures,  in conjunction with a 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> required documents produced  within the constraints required by your COI.

This is how the online part of our system works (but without the triggers as 
we still use Datacomm DB. I suspect if we move to DB2 we would use 
triggers.) We use MQM triggers and save the messages/transactions in a db 
and they are processed by "batch" CICS programs. By batch I mean the 
COBOL/CICS programs that are not associated with any terminal. At the end of 
the process replies are written to DB tables and MQM is triggered to send 
the replies back to the original sender. This approach does place some 
software development/acquisition burden on the organizations that interface 
with our system but we are the federal government and while they have input 
they don't have much choice.

Anyway we are able to process enough transactions quickly enough that the 
people who would like to us SAP to replace our system feel mightily 
challenged. The Java folks likewise.  There has been some talk of 
benchmarking Java against COBOL/CICS before deciding whether to use it to 
replace COBOL/CICS, but not much actual work has been done that I know of. I 
think they just want to be able to say that the old/obsolete COBOL has been 
replaced by the modern Java and I believe other factors do not matter too 
much. Well I could say a lot more but every time I start on this I remind 
myself that I am just short of retirement and I self censor myself. Besdies 
whatever their shortcomings it is still my duty to support management 
decisions.

We still have a few batch programs e.g. one copies old data from production 
to an archive system, something users probably would not like to do one at a 
time.

I am eligible to retire as of June this year and my financial advisor says I 
can afford to do it as soon as 2008. I am thinking of trying to last until 
Jan. 3, 2014 but I will just see how things go. I look forward to doing the 
projects that I want to do for a change.
```

You may be right. I concede that it is arguable.

All of the examples cited above could be produced as by-products of 
transactional processing. Payrolls, certainly are good examples of a batch 
process, but the same effect can be obtained by time-triggered processes 
against a database.

(Imagine a payroll system where a stored procedure is triggered 
automatically to calculate the base pay for an individual,  and the tax to 
be paid, and transactions that make adjustments to this have been enqueued 
against the individual account. The whole process will run automatically and 
all the paymaster has to do is provide the adjustment transactions in real 
time. It doesn't HAVE to be batch, although, in essence, it is a 
"batch-like" process, inasmuch as many people will have their pay 
calculations trggered at the same time...)

Stored procedures and Data Warehousing, together, can obviate all the batch 
processes I can think of. (I may have missed some... :-))  Printing cheques 
will soon be a thing of the past; direct transfers and smart cards are more 
secure and more convenient for the drawer and the payee.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Productivity

_(thread depth: 8)_

- **From:** Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2007-01-02T08:05:33-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<spskp2trujv9racfriqbphc4l384s0gurs@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<4v33ipF1a006cU1@mid.individual.net> <73e30$4592adbc$454920f8$28313@KNOLOGY.NET> <4vgl8dF1bmbn5U1@mid.individual.net> <dnn7p2lh7524u11iuvt77dcchh76c53cfk@4ax.com> <4vis2tF1cbkkdU1@mid.individual.net> <pQDlh.78496$V34.25214@newsfe17.lga> <4vpn2rF1cr7poU1@mid.individual.net> <1167574958.426550.272040@n51g2000cwc.googlegroups.com>`

```
Typically batch programs are useful when we want the job to run for a
particular moment in time.

Closing, payroll, billing, grades, etc. need to be run for particular
times.

Traditionally, we made sure transactions would not get posted while we
ran these period-end jobs.     But more and more, we do not want to do
this.   When someone pays us via the Web in the middle of the night,
we want him to check his new balance.

One solution is to have a duplicate of our data that they can see
their updates - and merge the two databases when we are done.

Of course data warehouses can carry this duplication to the extreme.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Productivity

_(thread depth: 9)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2007-01-03T10:47:32+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<50027mF1cs5dgU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<4v33ipF1a006cU1@mid.individual.net> <73e30$4592adbc$454920f8$28313@KNOLOGY.NET> <4vgl8dF1bmbn5U1@mid.individual.net> <dnn7p2lh7524u11iuvt77dcchh76c53cfk@4ax.com> <4vis2tF1cbkkdU1@mid.individual.net> <pQDlh.78496$V34.25214@newsfe17.lga> <4vpn2rF1cr7poU1@mid.individual.net> <1167574958.426550.272040@n51g2000cwc.googlegroups.com> <spskp2trujv9racfriqbphc4l384s0gurs@4ax.com>`

```

"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message 
news:spskp2trujv9racfriqbphc4l384s0gurs@4ax.com...
> Typically batch programs are useful when we want the job to run for a
> particular moment in time.
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> Of course data warehouses can carry this duplication to the extreme.

Yes, more and more people want to see how things are NOW. If the computer 
system models the real world, and deals with events as they occur, I believe 
this can be achieved, without batch processing. Sure there are time points 
where we want to see consolidation, and there are historical and traditional 
reasons (like monthly billing) where people are used to seeing the state of 
their account and so forth, that press system designers to produce the goods 
at a certain time. But it doesn't HAVE to be that way. Since going to online 
statements from my bank, for example, I haven't felt compelled to check my 
balance at the end of each month. Instead, I can check it daily  (24/7) or 
whenever I feel it needs checking...

Provided you have security and backups in place, I see no reason why you 
should stop Transaction Processing to get figures for a given moment in 
time. Instead, simply schedule a trigger for that moment (maybe weeks, 
months or even years in advance and set to repeat at whatever cycle you 
want) and let the system tell you the state of things at that moment. I 
wouldn't run duplicate databases (except in the sense of RAIDing them, which 
is transparent to the process) and I see no reason why customers can't see 
their balance in real time on the real database. It is only because the 
batch process takes time, that it is necessary to stop processing while it 
goes on. If everything was accrued already there'd be no need for a batch 
process...:-)

I realise that with existing systems in place it is not likely that 
something like this could be attained; the real world requires real 
solutions, but the concept could be used for future design.

I honestly believe that things will evolve this way, but time will tell...

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Productivity

_(thread depth: 7)_

- **From:** "P. Raulerson" <paul.rl@raulersons.com>
- **Date:** 2007-01-01T15:22:28-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<rYemh.19469$ky2.5651@newsfe21.lga>`
- **References:** `<4v33ipF1a006cU1@mid.individual.net> <73e30$4592adbc$454920f8$28313@KNOLOGY.NET> <4vgl8dF1bmbn5U1@mid.individual.net> <dnn7p2lh7524u11iuvt77dcchh76c53cfk@4ax.com> <4vis2tF1cbkkdU1@mid.individual.net> <pQDlh.78496$V34.25214@newsfe17.lga> <4vpn2rF1cr7poU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
<grin> Well., you are welcome to your opinion Pete, and you might even be 
right. However, I think you are looking at it from an idealized viewpoint. 
I;ve worked with some very large data warehouses, and the input data always 
requires some kind of preprocessing. In the scenario you are talking about, 
I'm not sure what you have is a real data warehouse and not some kind of 
hybrid OLTP system with a very large database on the backend.

Batch systems are rarely 'brute force' systems these days, and almost always 
operate on a set of transactions. Operations occur only on a subset of the 
main datastore, and even then the process is usually streamlined in terms of 
queries or other data access.

When you look at it from an Ops manager point of view, a batch process does 
not have to deal with transaction processing overhead, and you can usually 
eliminate any slowdowns  - like filesharing. There are many other reasons of 
course, but in simple fact, a tuned batch process *is* just that - tuned to 
do what it is designed to do with maximum speed and minimum resources.

And when you are putting stuff in a processing window, speed counts more 
than anything else except accuracy. Another advantage is that a batch system 
can run without affecting the online systems, leaving them up and able to 
process at full speed, or at best, down for only a short period of time 
while batch data is being gathered or organized.

Throwing hardware at this problem quickly reaches limits - either 
technological or financial. Usually financial. ;)  However well designed 
highly efficient batch processing is doable, can be easily controlled, is 
not difficult to expand, and is still faster.

-Paul


"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:4vpn2rF1cr7poU1@mid.individual.net...
>
> "P. Raulerson" <paul.rl@raulersons.com> wrote in message 
…[83 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

> always requires some kind of preprocessing. In the scenario you are 
> talking about, I'm not sure what you have is a real data warehouse and not 
> some kind of hybrid OLTP system with a very large database on the backend.
>

Well the people running it are DW experts and THEY think it is a DW... I 
don't have enough expertise in this particular area to argue one way or the 
other. (I was very interested to find out something about this and their use 
of pivot tables and cubes...).

Whenever these guys do a presentation to the rest of the company, showing 
some new facility they have developed, it simply leaves everyone stunned... 
they are VERY good at what they do.

> Batch systems are rarely 'brute force' systems these days, and almost 
> always operate on a set of transactions. Operations occur only on a subset 
> of the main datastore, and even then the process is usually streamlined in 
> terms of queries or other data access.

OK, I won't argue that one. It probably varies form place to place.

>
> When you look at it from an Ops manager point of view, a batch process 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> minimum resources.

But, if you could eliminate it altogether, wouldn't you?

>
> And when you are putting stuff in a processing window, speed counts more 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> time while batch data is being gathered or organized.

So why is the Batch window usually overnight, when the online system is not 
running? Could it be because batch processing has been found to interfere so 
severely with online processing that it simply isn't viable to run the 
online system concurrently with it? Obviously, this will depend entirely on 
the volumes of transactions and the amount of batch processing, so, again, 
it will vary from place to place.
>
> Throwing hardware at this problem quickly reaches limits - either 
> technological or financial. Usually financial. ;)  However well designed 
> highly efficient batch processing is doable, can be easily controlled, is 
> not difficult to expand, and is still faster.

I think we have to agree to disagree here :-). It is pretty academic anyway, 
because the world will vote with its feet and time will tell what is most 
viable.

Pete.
>
> -Paul

```

"P. Raulerson" <paul.rl@raulersons.com> wrote in message 
news:YNmmh.28895$_44.14368@newsfe23.lga...
> Comments interspersed below...
>
<snip>>>>
>>> When you look at it from an Ops manager point of view, a batch process 
>>> does not have to deal with transaction processing overhead, and you can 
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>

So, then it couldn't be eliminated. My question was, if it COULD be 
eliminated... :-)

Maybe the degree of "could' depends on how badly you want to do it...



>>>
>>> And when you are putting stuff in a processing window, speed counts more 
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> any kind of interactive processing was expensive.

There was a time (and I remember being part of it) when batch processing was 
the ONLY option. There WERE no online transactions. So I understand what you 
are saying. However, new advances in hardware (the provision of random 
access facilities, for a start...) enabled new approaches to be taken, and 
they were.

If you are dealing with systems like these, which certainly had OLTP "tacked 
on" to them, then I can see why you believe the way you do.

The fact is that OLTP systems designed from the ground up CAN be built 
without need for batch processing.  But it will be sometime before people 
realise they can... :-)


>And the systems are large Pete - even a very fast PC would not be suitable 
>for a lot of this kind of processing. Not because the raw processing power 
>isn't there, but because when you add the I/O systems, backup, security, 
>encryption, and Transaction Processing capability, they come up missing on 
>a a PC platform.

I strongly disagree on this... these things are certainly NOT missing on a 
PC platform. Your mainframe salesman just wants you to think they are... :-)

1. The power of the "PC platform" is not in its hardware but in its 
connectivity. It is the power of the NETWORK with distributed processing, 
remote procedure calls, load levelling and very fast message switching, that 
empowers the OLTP system. All of the things you mention above are no longer 
the preserve of the mainframe; any good client/server networked system will 
include them.

2. Networked systems built around the "PC platform" are scalable. And for 
much less money than the cost of scaling a centralized mainframe. For 
mainframes to survive, the cost of ownership will have to declne 
dramatically, and the system environment will need to become much simpler.

That has been my experience, but I accept that it is arguable and views will 
differ, depending on what you're used to and familiar with. My experience 
covers decades in both environments. I am persuaded that the role of 
mainframes in the future will be as servers on the network and Batch 
processors until everyone wises up... :-)


> By PC I am referring to the personal machines we all use - not Intel based 
> mainframes like Unisys puts out.
>

My points above were deliberately constrained to meet those criteria, with 
the exception that some of these platforms will be servers...

>
>>> Throwing hardware at this problem quickly reaches limits - either 
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> :)

We ain't seen nothin' yet... :-)

Pete.
```

Pete Dashwood wrote:
>
> 2. Networked systems built around the "PC platform" are scalable. And for
> much less money than the cost of scaling a centralized mainframe. For
> mainframes to survive, the cost of ownership will have to declne
> dramatically, and the system environment will need to become much simpler.

People have been comparing the cost of ownership between mainframe and
Unix systems, using scalability as an argument for downsizing to
smaller boxes for years. I have twice watched users attempt to downsize
to Unix boxes (advised by the salemen that they were powerful enough to
handle 2 million product lines) and fall on their swords in the
process. WRT Unix boxes, once ancilliary services are taken into
account, I believe that the Unix box will prove to be less reliable and
more expensive (I may be out of date by now).

Atari's disk drive).    It didn't have the capacity of a big mainframe
or Supercomputer running Unix.    

Deciding what next computer will do the job is not much different than
it ever was.

```

"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:4vulmlF1e1bngU1@mid.individual.net...
>
> "P. Raulerson" <paul.rl@raulersons.com> wrote in message 
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>

<grin> Remind to tell you a funny story about having to argue with IBM to 
get them to sell me a mainframe a few years ago...
I won. :)

> 1. The power of the "PC platform" is not in its hardware but in its 
> connectivity. It is the power of the NETWORK with distributed processing, 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>
This is true, but it is not always true in all situations. Networks have 
latency issues, and queue issues, and a whole bunch of issues that we all 
battled with when Sun came up with 'the network IS the computer'. In other 
words, I don't disagree entirely.

> 2. Networked systems built around the "PC platform" are scalable. And for 
> much less money than the cost of scaling a centralized mainframe. For 
> mainframes to survive, the cost of ownership will have to declne 
> dramatically, and the system environment will need to become much simpler.
>
Oh - they *have*. For instance we run a mainframe with no full time system 
programmer - just little old me. Of course, it mainly runs Linux instances, 
but run it does and very well. And cheaply too!  You can also buy mainframes 
now that are dedicated to running particular types of things - for example 
with a processor that is tuned and optimized to run Java.

Revisting cost - you can pick up a really good mainframe system for $500K 
today, including SAN, Network, Install, OS, and three years of maintenance.

A Blade Server that is roughly equivalent, with SAN, runs closer to $730K. 
(Just bought one of them things, and I only bought 11 blades, not the full 
14. ) Software costs a LOT on the PC platforms.  In our case, about $1300 
per user for Microsoft software. That's without a database system other than 
Access.
Those darned CALS add up quick...

The big difference? Take Tivoli, for example. Tivoli can near max out a 
mainframe IFL processor without even trying, but it is a whole different 
thing trying to max out the channel I/O.  The PC is swimming in MIPS, but 
even in the newest IBM blade center chassis, has lots of trouble with I/O. 
Technically, the bandwidth is there, but compared to the mainframe, the 
components- Fibre Cards, DASD controllers, Network Controllers and such are 
poorly integrated. It really shows up too.

Yesterday I found problems on the Blade Fibre cards relating to the cards 
building up hughe queuing delays. That would simply not happen on FICON. 
(Okay, FICON is basically Token Ring on Glass - but boy, it can move data 
around.)


> That has been my experience, but I accept that it is arguable and views 
> will differ, depending on what you're used to and familiar with. My 
…[31 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

Hi Charlie,

comments below...


"Charles Hottel" <chottel@earthlink.net> wrote in message 
news:1kCmh.5782$pQ3.319@newsread4.news.pas.earthlink.net...
>
> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>
I downloaded Visual C# 2005 Express Edition  for FREE from: 
http://msdn.microsoft.com/vstudio/express/

It installs easily and the Visual Studio IDE comes up when you start it.

There is a "News" section on the start page and it has a link for "Absolute 
Beginners" I took that and started from lesson 01.

OK, for some of this course, there was a lot of stuff I already knew, but I 
tried to treat it as a new subject in a new unit of time without imposing 
ITSAs on it. As a result, I acquired of lot of useful knowledge in passing. 
These online tutorials are absolutely excellent. The base course has around 
14 hours that you can download or simply view, and the lessons are organized 
into easy pieces which I found were just the right length. In addition to 
the newbie course, there are over 400 videos you can view for free on all 
aspects of the language and the environment.

The next thing is to get stuck in and start using it. I found the help files 
to be comprehensive and the How To sections are very useful. It is really 
nice to have help that offers sample code, in the language you are actually 
writing (ever had to translate VB examples into COBOL?  :-))

If you have experience with OO and particularly JavaScript, it probably 
helps, but these are certainly not pre-requisites.

So, that's it for me... no books, no video purchases, real quality tuition 
on-line when I want it, excellent reference and examples online when I want 
them, and all for free... It is no wonder this language is taking off...

Funny thing is, if Fujitsu had sold me DotNET COBOL for several thousand 
dollars, I probably wouldn't even have looked at C#. Now I'm really glad 
they didn't... Sometimes things just work out :-)

Pete.

```
I'm responding to this message instead of the one you directed to me.

Pete Dashwood<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> 12/31/06 5:00 AM >>>
>
>
>(Consider an online transaction that calculates interest on an account
which 
>last had a balance change 3 weeks ago. The transaction can access a 
>collection of interest rates for the dates between then and today and 
>calculate the accrued interest; it only has to calculate for one period
(the 
>time since the last balance chage). It "knows" the balance changed because

>it is the transaction that changed it, and it wouldn't be running UNLESS
the 
>balance changed...

If I'm reading this, and your message to me, correctly are you saying that
accrued interest is only calculated when either 1) a transactions posts or
2) it's time to pay the interest to the account?

In our environment we not only accrue interest to accounts during a daily
batch process, but we also generate combined general ledger transactions
representing the interest accrued each day for all accounts.  This, I
believe, is so that on any given day one can look at the "interest owed" GL
account and know how much interest we owe our customers, but we have not yet
actually paid to them.

Can you work this in to your "online processing"?  (Probably you can!)

I'm also still unsure as to how you would handle statement processing.  Not
the regular transactions themselves, but all of the things that are done
when the 'statement cycles', such as paying accrued interest in to the
account, assessing a monthly service charge based on numbers of transactions
during the statement period, etc.  How does the "cycle statement today"
event get triggered?  At the very least, it seems to me, there has to be a
daily event of some sort that says "trigger the 'cycle statement today'
event for all accounts scheduled to cycle today".  Seems to me that that
would "flood" your online system with what is essentailly a batch process.

Anyway, we too have an online banking site where you can see transactions
that have posted up through the last business day.  And we even have "up to
the second" pending transactions available for things like ATM and Debit
Card transactions, telephone and online initiated account transfers, etc.

Frank


--- 
Frank Swarbrick
Senior Developer/Analyst - Mainframe Applications
FirstBank Data Corporation - Lakewood, CO  USA

Quick comments below...

"Frank Swarbrick" <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com> wrote in message

…[6 more quoted lines elided]…

Yes, batch processing is good in this case. My argument is not that it is
useful; my argument is that it doesn't HAVE to be that way. If your
underlying systems were designed differently I believe it could be obviated.
Even with what you have, given a transaction rate of 70 per second (these
are fairly typical top end ORACLE figures), it would take 4 hours to process
1,000,000 customers. But this 4 hours can be spread over 24; it isn't a
"window". The transaction system runs 24/7.
>
> Can you work this in to your "online processing"?  (Probably you can!)

Easily. GL update would be a triggered stored procedure.

>
> I'm also still unsure as to how you would handle statement processing.
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Yes, it would. (see above). And doing it in batch is a viable solution...
for now. The point is that if the system were designed from scratch, the
exceptional transaction loads would be catered for.

I believe this is the way that systems will be and are evolving. The network
reflects real events; the system models the real world and that model
includes real time.

But right now... batch processing serves us well.

- **From:** "Alistair" <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk>

- **Message-ID:** `<1167651660.706212.71290@s34g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>`

- **References:** `<4v33ipF1a006cU1@mid.individual.net> <73e30$4592adbc$454920f8$28313@KNOLOGY.NET> <4vgl8dF1bmbn5U1@mid.individual.net> <dnn7p2lh7524u11iuvt77dcchh76c53cfk@4ax.com> <9493c$4597e17f$454920f8$31548@KNOLOGY.NET>`

```

LX-i wrote:
>
> Batch COBOL could still be used to deal with the data in the warehouse,
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>

There are overheads with non-compiled languages. The classic example is
Basic with the interpreter being very slow (GW-Basic and MSBasic). Some
other languages interpret the queries once only (ie compile on the fly)
and have smaller overheads. I would love to see exactly how a modern
query language performs with a massive query (large volumes of data and
large numbers of fields/cells).
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Productivity

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2007-01-02T11:31:10+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4vtgdhF1cvbimU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<4v33ipF1a006cU1@mid.individual.net> <73e30$4592adbc$454920f8$28313@KNOLOGY.NET> <4vgl8dF1bmbn5U1@mid.individual.net> <dnn7p2lh7524u11iuvt77dcchh76c53cfk@4ax.com> <9493c$4597e17f$454920f8$31548@KNOLOGY.NET> <1167651660.706212.71290@s34g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>`

```

"Alistair" <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote in message 
news:1167651660.706212.71290@s34g2000cwa.googlegroups.com...
>
> LX-i wrote:
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> Basic with the interpreter being very slow (GW-Basic and MSBasic).

Perhaps it is some time since you used these? There is nothing slow about 
modern interpreted Basic, running on a modern processor.  Visual Basic is 
compiled nowadays.


> Some
> other languages interpret the queries once only (ie compile on the fly)
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Have you tried running SQL queries against an ACCESS database on your own 
system?  Access 2003 performs very well. (It uses the SQLServer engine). I 
use MySQL and find the SQL runs very acceptably. Queries that return 
thousands of rows still run very quickly. (A query that returned 50,000 rows 
just ran in under 3 seconds, and that includes all the overheads of setup 
and connection; had the DBMS already been loaded it would have been much 
faster.)

I'm currently using C# to access databases using OLEDB and the Dot NET 
framework. A connection is made, the query runs, and results are loaded into 
a dataset, then the connection is dropped and processing proceeds with the 
local dataset. The whole thing (for the queries I have done so far, which 
are not extreme), appears to be instantaneous. However I am running on dual 
core2 64 processors so it may not be representative of things in general.

The point is that with modern processors and modern DBMS, performance is 
much less of a problem than it was a few years ago.

The approaches are different now as well. Instead of cursor processing 
through the DBMS  for queries that return many rows, the whole thing is 
loaded to a result set which is an object with its own methods and 
properties. These can be invoked without even affecting the DBMS at all, so 
stepping forward and backward or even randomly into the result set is 
achieved on the local copy. The DBMS is free to service other requests. If 
update is required it is done by a method of the result set and if 
reconnection is necessary, the result set object does it automatically, 
applies the update, and disconnects again, all transparent to the 
application program.Connections are established and held for a fraction of 
the time that used to be the case.(You can force the connection to persist 
if you have some pressing reason to do so; so far, I have been very happy 
with the results of letting the object manage this for me.)

I remember having a hard time here some years back, attempting to persuade 
people that DBMS was a better solution than the COBOL file system. My main 
reason for believing that was because the COBOL file system is closed; you 
need to write a COBOL program to access data with it. The DBMS is open and 
can be imported and exported to other standard tools. With increasing need 
for data to be manipulated and shared openly, DBMS has established itself as 
the repository of choice. The main "objections" (usually from COBOL people 
who feel threatened by having a non-COBOL solution...) have been 
"performance" and "overhead".  In today's environment, neither of these is a 
real problem; even embedded SQL (which was a much better solution than 
ISAM/VSAM KSDS file systems), is now being superseded by Object Oriented 
DBMS handling, as outlined above.

I just had a look through some of my stuff and the last time I used an 
Indexed Sequential file was 12 years ago...:-)

Pete.
```

There are circumstances in which a DBMS solution would be better but
all too often people choose technological solutions that look good on
the cv/have sexy desktops/allow unfettered user access/are the latest
buzzwords, etc. Just because it looks good, smells good and tastes
good, doesn't mean that it can do the job. A colleague of mine (that
drunken bum who had the problem with referring to the same dataset
twice in one program) says that all of his mainframe problems originate
in sexy excel or unix systems where the users are allowed to do
anything they want (including re-arranging spreadsheet columns).

over time. Cobol systems I have worked on could also be utilised by
Selcopy, Assembler, Basic, etc., programs. Your argument can also be
applied against Java sourced files, etc.

The prime reasons for a DBMS must be the facts that it is a managed
system and permits access to data and facilities in a manner that
sequential and indexed files do not. FTR, I like Access. I can't wait
for it to catch up with Adabas and get Multiple Value Fields. Oh that
it would get Periodic Groups as well. And Sub-descriptors too. It is
terrible how slowly that pc technology is catching up with mainframe
technology ;-)

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Productivity

_(thread depth: 8)_

- **From:** "Oliver Wong" <owong@castortech.com>
- **Date:** 2007-01-02T15:32:29-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<xjzmh.63598$Rg5.539356@weber.videotron.net>`
- **References:** `<4v33ipF1a006cU1@mid.individual.net> <73e30$4592adbc$454920f8$28313@KNOLOGY.NET> <4vgl8dF1bmbn5U1@mid.individual.net> <dnn7p2lh7524u11iuvt77dcchh76c53cfk@4ax.com> <9493c$4597e17f$454920f8$31548@KNOLOGY.NET> <1167651660.706212.71290@s34g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <4vtgdhF1cvbimU1@mid.individual.net> <1167762300.998062.22940@42g2000cwt.googlegroups.com>`

```

"Alistair" <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote in message 
news:1167762300.998062.22940@42g2000cwt.googlegroups.com...
>I guess that you've recovered from the hangover.
>
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> Glad to hear it. (no sarcasm intended)

    All things being equal, a program running via an interpreter will be 
slower than the same program running pre-compiled. However...

    (1) In real life scenarios, for user applications at least (I'm not that 
familiar with the batch processing world), the CPU is typically idle, 
waiting for the user to do something. For example, my newsgroup client is 
spending most of its time, idly waiting for me to type the next character in 
my reply-post; And so processing time isn't such a big deal. We have plenty.

    (2) "All things" are not equal: A program which is interpreted can take 
advantage of information which is available at runtime, but not which was 
not available at compile time, to make better optimization decisions. Some 
program designs can take advantage of this a lot, with the interpreted 
program running faster than the compiled one. Others, not so much, and so 
the compiled program will run faster than the interpreted one.

[...]
> I can't wait for the quantum computer
> to become a reality - every answer instantly.

    Actually, there's a fairly limited class of problems for which we know 
how to program a quantum computers to solve instantly (i.e. quantum 
computers may be good for other problems as well, but we haven't figured out 
how to use them more efficiently than classical computers yet):

http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_computing
<quote>
   1. The only way to solve it is to guess answers repeatedly and check 
them,
   2. There are n possible answers to check,
   3. Every possible answer takes the same amount of time to check, and
   4. There are no clues about which answers might be better: generating 
possibilities randomly is just as good as checking them in some special 
order.

An example of this is a password cracker that attempts to guess the password 
for an encrypted file
</quote>

    And even then, there are still limitations in RAM, harddisk and network 
speeds.

    - Oliver
```

On PC and UNIX systems, indexed files and record sequential files are
add-ons.  On IBM mainframes and I would assume those from other
manufacturers, what you refer to as the COBOL file system is actually
a native file system open and available to all tools on the box in
question.  Indeed, on the IBM MVS / z/OS systems, COBOL doesn't take
full advantage of the capabilities of the underlying file system and
in some cases, assembler sub-routines are needed.  Utilities
(sometimes I called them futilities) such as sort and various copy
programs, virtually all of the third party products such as DYL280 and
Easytrieve,  and virtually all of the languages had native access. The
one major brain dead exception was that the interactive tool -
TSO/ISPF steadfastly refused to recognize VSAM.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL file system was Re: Productivity

_(thread depth: 8)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2007-01-07T15:02:44+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<50b2m5F1f98mjU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<4v33ipF1a006cU1@mid.individual.net> <73e30$4592adbc$454920f8$28313@KNOLOGY.NET> <4vgl8dF1bmbn5U1@mid.individual.net> <dnn7p2lh7524u11iuvt77dcchh76c53cfk@4ax.com> <9493c$4597e17f$454920f8$31548@KNOLOGY.NET> <1167651660.706212.71290@s34g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <4vtgdhF1cvbimU1@mid.individual.net> <ra6tp2d4j8j7udbg1sdehncvn5clcub98l@4ax.com>`

```

"Clark F Morris" <cfmpublic@ns.sympatico.ca> wrote in message 
news:ra6tp2d4j8j7udbg1sdehncvn5clcub98l@4ax.com...
> On Tue, 2 Jan 2007 11:31:10 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> add-ons.

Not if you buy a COBOL compiler. COBOL standards require support for the 
"COBOL file system" (Not my words... check the COBOL manual :-))

> On IBM mainframes and I would assume those from other
> manufacturers, what you refer to as the COBOL file system is actually
> a native file system open and available to all tools on the box in
> question.

Yes, inasmuch as VSAM is provided and many of the standard tools can take 
advantage of it. COBOL just happens to use VSAM/KSDS for indexed sequential 
support and VSAM/RRDS for relative addressing support. (I dont think 
VSAM/ESDS is used by the COBOL file system, but I could be wrong and ESDS is 
certainly accessible form COBOL if you use extensions. This may have 
changed; it is around 20 years since I worked in that environment.) The 
relative byte addressing provided by ESDS remains a mystery to most COBOL 
programmers BECAUSE it is NOT part of the "COBOL file system".

No, inasmuch as there is no standard import and export of VSAM datasets into 
formats acceptable to applications not written in COBOL, PL-1, or Assembler, 
or in cross platform formats like XML or .CSV. (Again, this may have changed 
and perhaps some of these things may be availabe now; if so it was really 
too late... we needed it 20 years ago...:-))

Because RDBMS contain details of the structure of their data they have 
ALWAYS been able to import and export structure and data into formats 
accessible by virtually any application. (at the lowest level they can 
export SQL information as text; MySQL does this for both structure and 
content and can therefore easily be imported into any other RDBMS that 
supports standard SQL. (I don't know of any that don't... :-))

The closest approximation I can think of is using IDCAMS to export the data 
on a KSDS to a sequential file, but that is a long way from "open" access.

It is probably being a bit unfair to the mainframe environment to say the 
file formats are not open, because there is little requirement for 
"openness" on a central platform. It is only as the need for networking and 
remote computing capability  across different platforms becomes more 
important that "exchangeability" of data becomes a consideration. My 
argument was (and is...) that RDBMS supports this better than the COBOL file 
system. When I first proposed this it was heresy and I was castigated in 
this forum (I still remember it... :-)... I couldn't beleive the venom in 
some of the posts...), since then the world has voted with its feet and few 
applications DON'T use RDBMS (or Hierarchical or Network DBMS) on both 
manframes and PCs.

> Indeed, on the IBM MVS / z/OS systems, COBOL doesn't take
> full advantage of the capabilities of the underlying file system and
> in some cases, assembler sub-routines are needed.  Utilities
> (sometimes I called them futilities)

 :-) (I like that...)

> such as sort and various copy
> programs, virtually all of the third party products such as DYL280 and
> Easytrieve,  and virtually all of the languages had native access. The
> one major brain dead exception was that the interactive tool -
> TSO/ISPF steadfastly refused to recognize VSAM.

yeah, I know... :-) How crazy is that? (I think it is OK if you deal with 
cataloged sequential datasets... and GDSes... not sure now...)

Pete.
```

_(thread depth: 9)_

- **From:** Clark F Morris <cfmpublic@ns.sympatico.ca>

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<619dq29odei7fnplcj9ps0stp6q2er4te0@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<4v33ipF1a006cU1@mid.individual.net> <73e30$4592adbc$454920f8$28313@KNOLOGY.NET> <4vgl8dF1bmbn5U1@mid.individual.net> <dnn7p2lh7524u11iuvt77dcchh76c53cfk@4ax.com> <9493c$4597e17f$454920f8$31548@KNOLOGY.NET> <1167651660.706212.71290@s34g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <4vtgdhF1cvbimU1@mid.individual.net> <ra6tp2d4j8j7udbg1sdehncvn5clcub98l@4ax.com> <50b2m5F1f98mjU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Sun, 7 Jan 2007 15:02:44 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>
>"Clark F Morris" <cfmpublic@ns.sympatico.ca> wrote in message 
…[25 more quoted lines elided]…
>"COBOL file system" (Not my words... check the COBOL manual :-))

Here is an interesting situation.  For the mainframes I mentioned, the
COBOL file system was mapped to the standard file facilities and thus
was not viewed as a COBOL file system.  For PCs and UNIX based
systems, the COBOL file system became an add-on.  For the mainframe,
the basic FORTRAN and C style files were the oddballs (delimiter
terminated strings and arrays).  The reverse is true for the PC and
UNIX systems.
>
>> On IBM mainframes and I would assume those from other
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>programmers BECAUSE it is NOT part of the "COBOL file system".

The VSAM Entry Sequenced Data Set is accessible if the SELECT
statement is coded properly.  There are heretics like myself who
believe that it should have been enhanced over time so that it could
supplant the QSAM (queued sequential access method) data sets, that
you should be able to concatenate ESDS and QSAM data sets and that you
shouldn't need a special SELECT statement to use them.
>
>No, inasmuch as there is no standard import and export of VSAM datasets into 
…[25 more quoted lines elided]…
>manframes and PCs.

On a PC, the RDBMS is a better choice than adding a proprietary
indexed file system.  However, there are interesting drawbacks.  I was
surprised when testing something in DB2 on the mainframe to find that
I couldn't mix accessing production (or common test) tables in a read
only manner with the test tables I was interested in within the same
program.  It seems that all of the tables have to be accessed by the
same instance of the data base subsystem.  This becomes less of a
problem as DASD becomes cheaper but it still can add up if an
application requires a large number of tables.  I am used to just
changing the file that the DD (data set definition?) statement points
to when I want my own copy of a file.  While a RDBMS makes a number of
complex situations easier and probably is better for 99.9 percent of
the customer files, it makes simple table files where there is a key
and one or two data fields much more of a pain to handle.  I suspect
that in many cases, the RDBMS has become the 40 ton truck used where a
wheelbarrow would be perfectly adequate.  An RDBMS will have greater
overhead because access is not at the row level but at the column
level based on the generated call statements that I have seen.  This
allows for interesting compression and other techniques to minimize
disk and in storage buffer space at the cost of long access pat
lengths.  The cost can be more than justified if the suite of
applications using the data base is taking advantage of the RDBMS
capabilities as opposed to treating it is a tricky indexed file.    
>
>> Indeed, on the IBM MVS / z/OS systems, COBOL doesn't take
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>cataloged sequential datasets... and GDSes... not sure now...)

I think that ISPF without tweaking can handle UNIX file systems if the
user has UNIX access allowed in their security system definition. VSAM
still is primarily through third party products.  Of course, IBM needs
to understand that just because its first attempts at providing full
GUI workstation access received underwhelming acceptance in the field,
it is no reason to retreat from the idea of providing a good modern
IDE.  
>
>Pete. 
>
```

But, as the systems atrophy, there are users who see the system as, at 
best, useless - and, at worst, something that actually makes their job 
harder, instead of easier.  With money having to be split between 
moderately improving old systems and these new-and-improved 
"replacement" systems, who looks out for the users?

When I arrived here, we were "frozen" on new development.  We couldn't 
even fix big bugs, because a replacement system's requirements had been 
written on one version of software.  For over 3 years, there were no 
improvements to our system, and the users were howling.  Once that 
contract lapsed (120M USD later), we were able to make great strides. 
Did they "learn their lesson"?  Of course not.  Now we're hearing 
rumblings about the same thing happening again.

to do a loose design and code on as a training task for the guy who is 
going to be picking up most of my programs.

- **References:** `<1167402575.105051.161550@k21g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <KLDlh.78495$V34.56910@newsfe17.lga> <en74pi$8a7$1@reader2.panix.com> <eaHlh.4354$hr3.2518@newsfe24.lga>`

```

"P. Raulerson" <paul.rl@raulersons.com> wrote in message 
news:eaHlh.4354$hr3.2518@newsfe24.lga...
> Comon Doc - I said if they were loyal to *him*.
> It would seem in his old job, he was loyal to them without any 
> reciprocation.
> -Paul
>

Unfortunately, Paul, you can't know whether they are loyal to you until the 
crunch comes. I have seen (on more than several occasions) permanent 
employees who were totally "company men"... fiercely loyal to the company, 
worked there for more than 15 years (in one case it was 22 years),  loads of 
unpaid overtime, etc. and yet, when the company has a hard time, they get 
fired.

On one occasion, the company implemented Voluntary Redundancy and paid large 
golden handshakes to people who took it. My friend decided he would apply 
(having worked there for 16 years and achieved middle management status). 
They turned him down. ("We don't want YOU to leave"...). Six months later 
they fired him (and a number of others who had refused VR or been declined 
when they applied), with his holiday pay and a very small "loyalty 
bonus"....Yet all the "dead wood" walked and got paid handsomely for doing 
so.

Were they a bad company?  I've thought about this a lot over the years. I 
don't think they did what they did with malice; it was simply expediency.

Do the people you work for owe you a living? It is arguable.

I was interested to see that the OP on this said they "refused to retrain" 
him for web development.

Do we have a right to expect a company to train us? Certainly, if they need 
a particular skill and it isn't readily available. But if it is in good 
supply, why should they? They are not our parents, or our friends, or our 
family. (Despite what the CEO says at the Xmas party... :-)) They are simply 
a legally constituted entity with the stated purpose of making money. Help 
them do that, and they may give you some.

(Just because you have friends at work does not mean that the "company" is 
your friend... this is a popular misconception.)

I have a slightly different approach to this; I consider that MY skill set 
is MY responsibility.

As it is my primary means of survival, it is FAR too important to entrust to 
any company I may be working for. The only person who can ensure I have a 
full toolbox is me.  If  a company is prepared to contribute to my training 
costs, that's fantastic, but I certainly wouldn't expect them to (and I'm 
speaking now as if I were a permanent employee...which I was for 10 years 
out of a 40 year career).

The hard fact is that there are no jobs for life any more. (Actually, there 
haven't been for almost the last three decades.) It is a market place; you 
better be sure you have something attractive to sell in it if you don't want 
to go home hungry. Not acquiring skill because a company won't pay you to, 
is simply foolhardy and shortsighted. It is the same foolishness and myopia 
which says "They'll take care of us; we have the pension plan and the 
benefits, and John, the Boss, is a good friend...". Sooner or later myopia 
will hit you in the wallet.

(Please note I am NOT arguing here what companies SHOULD do...I am 
describing real behaviours I have observed.)

Get trained, then find a job that will enable you to recoup your training 
investment, if your current job won't. With the advent of the web, and 
universal access to it, it doesn't even cost loads of money to acquire 
skill.

(It does require self-discipline, time, and application, but you get paid 
for it when you sell your newly acquired skill, or use it to build something 
that will generate revenue.  I have spent the last 5 weeks, in my spare 
time, learning C#. It has cost me only time and effort. I'm using my newly 
acquired knowledge to write something I have already sold and, although it 
isn't huge, it is an income source that will more than repay the time I 
spent. Sure, when the summer's over I'll go and get a ":real job", but 
meantime, why shouldn't the "hobby" pay for itself?  If it helps, look on 
your learning as a hobby. One day the hobby may feed you...)

Information is available. If you don't take advantage of it, someone else, 
somewhere else, will do so and that person will have your job. It's as 
simple as that.

Pete.

P.S. the guy in the story above who was refused VR  emigrated with his wife 
to Australia. They have never looked back. Sometimes, when one door closes, 
another (better?) door opens.

<snipped>
```

"Alistair" <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote in message 
news:1167760662.482970.99670@a3g2000cwd.googlegroups.com...
> But a little more honest than having extensive conditions (as we do in
> the UK) where only two tiny clauses are enforcable in law (place of
> work? and period of notice?). Lavishly specified terms and conditions
> for compulsory redundancy often get truncated to the government
> specified minimum.

Oh, we have that here (USA), too.

It's usually buried in a paragraph titled "Severability" and basically 
states that if any terms and conditions in the agreement are unenforceable 
due to state or federal law, the rest of the agreement is not affected... 
the underlying premise of which is, of course, that some terms and 
conditions to which the parties have agreed may/will violate a state or 
federal law.

Many many states have all kinds of these bullshit laws. And my state - 
Wisconsin - frankly has enough bullshit employment laws for ten states. You 
just gotta love a government which says, "If you can't read what you just 
signed, don't worry, we will 'protect' you from your own ignorance or 
foolishness."

MCM

"Clark F Morris" <cfmpublic@ns.sympatico.ca> wrote in message

This was in another place (Germany) and another time (20 years ago).  The 
culture was not a litigious one; people simply expected to be dealt with 
fairly. The company concerned, was, and is, a household name. They have no 
stains on their reputation. (Having said that, I never owned stock in it; I 
did in some other German companies which I felt were paragons of social 
responsibility...:-))

(A case could certainly be made, but a counter case can also be 
made...something along the lines of:

1. VR is not guaranteed. The Company is asking for people who would be 
prepared to take it so they can assess the situation. There is neither legal 
nor moral requirement for it to be approved for everyone who indicates they 
want it.

2. People who refuse VR have not prejudiced their right to quit; merely the 
right to get a lump sum for doing so.

3. People who request VR are not assured of getting it. That is made clear 
before negotiations begin. (Some people didn't apply because they were 
afraid it would be interpreted as meaning they were not committed to the 
company.)

4. The company has always had a right to fire people, just as employees have 
always had a right to quit. (VR does not change these rights.)

Given they can afford a better lawyer than you can, would you be prepared to 
pursue this?

At the time they turned down his VR application, I don't believe they had 
any intention of firing him...it's just the way events transpired.

(As an aside, I also failed to have my contract renewed by the same company 
at around the same time. They laid off several hundred people; many of whom 
were devastated. Three years later they tried to recruit many of them back 
again, but it was too late. Most had either moved in search of work, or 
redeployed themselves into other companies. It is a market place.)

One can certainly make the case that they were trying to save money; that is 
their responsibility to their shareholders and, as far as I know in any 
jurisdiction I am aware of, not against the Law.

>>Were they a bad company?  I've thought about this a lot over the years. I
>>don't think they did what they did with malice; it was simply expediency.
>>
> In this case, there is an odor of fraudulent dealing.

Odours are tentative things and hard to present as evidence...:-)

Pete

## Thread: COBOL format definitions? — 8 this month / 14 total

_Thread started [2006-12](2006-12.md#t-2ba1071359)._

##### ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL format definitions?

- **From:** Michael Russell <Michael.Russell@msn.com>
- **Date:** 2007-01-01T09:05:21+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<xvudnZGIbIxOUwXYnZ2dnUVZ8tmhnZ2d@pipex.net>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<12pbeo9ptfiuk43@news.supernews.com>`
- **References:** `<1167413507.056594.230340@k21g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <12pbeo9ptfiuk43@news.supernews.com>`

```
Top post:

  ... and remember the inherited usages ...

e.g. if a level 05 contains level 07s & the 07s don't have 
specific usages, they inherit from the 05
(is that quite right?)

'usage' = display, comp, comp-2, comp-3, comp-5, comp-x ...
if no 'usage' is declared, it's implicitly 'display', which 
means one defined 'character' = 1 platform's byte (how else to 
put that?)
'level' is a way of specifying a hierarchy of definitions of data.

e.g.

05  Name-and-Address.
     07  Name         pic x(20).
     07  Address-1    pic x(20).
     07  Zip          pic x(7).    or whatever.
I'm sure you probably understand that.

So, in the following, all the higher-numbered levels are comp-3:

05  Numeric-data  comp-3.
     07  First-numeric   pic 99.
     07  Second-numeric  pic s9(3)v99.

... more or less ... but there are odd instances where you 
begin to wonder, depending on the compiler!

hth

Michael

HeyBub wrote:
> Barb wrote:
>> Hi all,
…[68 more quoted lines elided]…
>

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL format definitions?

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
- **Date:** 2007-01-01T10:26:28-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<12pia62r53e2711@corp.supernews.com>`
- **References:** `<1167413507.056594.230340@k21g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <12pbeo9ptfiuk43@news.supernews.com> <xvudnZGIbIxOUwXYnZ2dnUVZ8tmhnZ2d@pipex.net> <1167657824.616060.319740@42g2000cwt.googlegroups.com>`

```

"Robert Jones" <rjones0@hotmail.com> wrote in message
news:1167657824.616060.319740@42g2000cwt.googlegroups.com...
[snip]
> I believe that specifying the USAGE at the group level for COBOL 85 was
> an IBM extension, they may not have been the only ones.  I think the
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> corrections.

The LRM for IBM ANS COBOL (68) does not
show it as an extension. The LRM for Micro Focus
COBOL 3.2 (74 and 85) does not show it as an
extension. The FDIS for ISO/IEC 1989:2002 COBOL
does not show it as a substantive change from the
previous ISO standard. These references seem to
suggest that "specifying the USAGE at the group level"
has been standard COBOL since the first official standard.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL format definitions?

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** "Robert Jones" <rjones0@hotmail.com>
- **Date:** 2007-01-01T09:50:13-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1167673813.778785.161840@v33g2000cwv.googlegroups.com>`

```
message snipped

Yes I was wrong, it was in the 1985 standard, see 5.14 The USAGE
clause, 5.14.4 GR 1. on page VI-46.  I don't know why I thought
otherwise!!
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL format definitions?

```
I was wrong, the impetus to find my 1985 COBOL standard confirms that
it is specified in 5.14 The USAGE Clause as 5.14.4 General Rules (1).
Don't know why I thought otherwise!!  I personally have usually used it
at the group level through a variety of IBM compilers.

apologies if this message appears twice, I posted one earlier that
seems to have disappeared.
```

#### ↳ Re: COBOL format definitions?

- **From:** "George" <georgech@hkstar.com>
- **Date:** 2007-01-10T23:53:20-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1168502000.171343.325170@o58g2000hsb.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<1167413507.056594.230340@k21g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<1167413507.056594.230340@k21g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>`

```
Dear Barb,

PIC S9(09) COMP-3 means 999999999 with sign and in computational-3
mode. Computational-3 is equalivent to packed-decimal. You can
translate the packed-decimal field into normal numeric field by the
following COBOL statements:

   01  PACKED-NUM S9(09) COMP-3.
   01  NORMAL-NUM S9(09).
   PROCEDURE DIVISION.
   ...
   MOVE PACKED-NUM TO NORMAL-NUM.

PIC 9(02)V9(04) means 99.9999.

PIC S9(07)V99 COMP-3 means 9999999.99 with sign and in computational-3
mode.

Yours sincerely,
George

Barb 寫道：

> Hi all,
>
…[28 more quoted lines elided]…
> Barb
```

```


docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
> (snip) 
> Ahhhhh... so instead of being ISO- (or whoever deems it-) compliant and 
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

Thanks for the suggestion, Doc.  And also thanks to Clark F Morris who 
suggested a similar solution.

One thing we do differently is we place the indexed element on the 
left side of the equal sign, like this:

SEARCH ALL COPYBOOK-STATE-TBL
     WHEN COPYBOOK-STATE-CODE(ST-IDX) = INFILE-CODE(1:2)

I wanted to keep it this way, to make it obvious just what indexed 
item is being searched, although I haven't tested it to determine if 
there is a performance difference.  It's possible to do reference 
modification on an indexed/subscripted data item, but I dislike the 
number of parentheses involved.  It just looks too complicated.

With kindest regards,
```

"Arnold Trembley" <arnold.trembley@worldnet.att.net> wrote in message

> One thing we do differently is we place the indexed element on the left 
> side of the equal sign, like this:
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I'm surprised to hear you say that, Arnold.

Refmodded indexing is a very useful tool and is less complex even than using 
a multi-dimensional array.

(See the original sample program I posted under the "productivity" thread 
for an example of refmodded indexing. (try a search on NVPARM)).

Everything looks strange and complicated until you become familiar with it. 
If you never become familiar with it, you are simply depriving your toolbox 
of powerful tools. It's like using a Mediaeval auger when you could have a 
cordless drill...

The whole reason I posted the code (which I VERY rarely do in CLC) is 
because Frank stated that he hoped he would never use a complex PERFORM. (I 
don't think he would have any trouble using one, and his first attempt at a 
complex UNSTRING was excellent.)

Complexity and complication is all relative. The answer is education and 
practice, not standards that emasculate the COBOL language.

Pete.
```

I went back and found your example, which included a line with 
indexing and reference modification:

MOVE INPUT-DATA (INPUT-DATA-POS:1) TO ARG-NAME (ARGS-X1) (J:1)

This is neither encouraged nor prohibited in my shop standards, and 
I've only seen it used in one production program.  It's more of a 
personal style preference on my part.  I'm not saying I would never 
use this construct, but rather that I prefer to accomplish the same 
effect without refmodding an indexed item (if I can), simply because 
it seems more readable to me without so many parentheses.

I can see where it has advantages in your example.

> practice, not standards that emasculate the COBOL language.
> 
> Pete.

I don't think a shop standard should prohibit refmodding an indexed 
item (or using SEARCH ALL).  But I would have no problem with a shop 
standard that prohibited comparing unequal length data items.  It's 
still pretty hard to catch that in a code walkthrough.

```
On Wed, 27 Dec 2006 12:54:09 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>I have a question, please, Doc.
>
…[35 more quoted lines elided]…
>>>> dealing with a fixed length dataset which has different-length records.

I (Clark Morris) interpreted the request as one that dealt with an
input file that was fixed block but depending on the day could have
different record lengths.  RECORD 0, BLOCK 0 handles that for input
with the interesting caveat that there must be  something in the data
that defines how long the actual record is (records with "A" in
position 1 are always 80 bytes while records with "B" in position 1
are always 3000 bytes).  When doing this the code must never attempt
to address parts of the record that are not there.  If the actual
record is only 80 bytes, the code actually working with that record
must not address the 200th byte.

>>
>> [snip]
…[26 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

DD,
  I wasn't one of the people who suggested this code (or approach) but do you 
get the same ABEND if your run-time JCL has:

//TESTA     DD  DSN=USERID.TESTA,DISP=SHR,DCB=(LRECL=32767,BLKSIZE=32767)

I *know* that your IEFBR14 created it with

//            DCB=(RECFM=FB,LRECL=30,BLKSIZE=27990)

but I *do* have a vague memory of being able to override LRECL via a DCB for an 
INPUT file.  If this approach DOES work, then the next question would be if it 
would work with a "real" input file rather than just a "marker" file created

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Variable Length Input File

- **From:** "coot_jg" <breany@csc.com>

```

William M. Klein wrote:
> DD,
>   I wasn't one of the people who suggested this code (or approach) but do you
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>

   Just out of curiosity, we were previously able to solve this - but I
am not aware as to whether the solution is still implementable.  The
input file records were created as fixed - (no leading length attribute
on each record/block) - and all records within a particular file had an
identical length, so we asked the console (or a parameter file/card)
for the required length(s). Prior to issuing an "OPEN" for the FD, we
issued a "CALL" to the COBOL program "TAMPER", passing the FD name  and
the record length(s) as  parameters.
   The "TAMPER" routine received a pointer to the DCB of the calling
program as it's first parameter, and was able to set the parameters
within that DCB to match the record length/block sizes to the expected
values, and pass the modified DCB back to the calling program for a
successful "OPEN".

    If this isn't valid anymore, I'd be curious as to the reason.

Thanx in advance.
```

"coot_jg" <breany@csc.com> wrote in message 
news:1167751524.129289.7460@n51g2000cwc.googlegroups.com...
>
> William M. Klein wrote:
<snip>
>   Just out of curiosity, we were previously able to solve this - but I
> am not aware as to whether the solution is still implementable.  The
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
>

As an IBM extension, one may pass the FD-name to a subroutine *IF* the file is a 
"QSAM" (I think this also works for SAM on VSE, but won't swear to it) file. 
This passes the address of the DCB, so a subprogram (usually - but not required 
to be - Assembler) can "play" with the attributes.  With OS/VS COBOL, one could 
also pass the FD-name of a VSAM file to get the ACB accessed in a subprogram. 
This is NO LONGER valid syntax (and will get a compile error) with all IBM 
compilers since VS COBOL II.

I think I suggested this approach early on (when we still thought the original 
poster was paying attention <G>)
```

```

lbaker@sboa.in.gov wrote:
> I assume the root user is runing the cron.  How do you run a cron job
> under your normal user?

What I do is log in as required user and run 'crontab -e' to
create/edit the cron file. You may need to edit the cron file
separately and then 'crontab filename' while logged in as the user.

See your system's documentation, or 'man crontab'.
```

```

lbaker@sboa.in.gov wrote:

> So are you saying that whatever user you are logged in as when you edit
> the cron tab file is the one that is logged in when it runs the job?

Actually each user has their own cron file, at least on Linux and
UnixWare it does. The actual cron files are in /var/spool/cron/ with a
file for each user.

> I will try editing the crontab logged in as my normal user and see if
> that makes any difference.

You won't edit *the* crontab, you will need to create a new one for
that user and edit the root cron file to remove lines.
```

## Thread: Move text to num problem — 5 msgs

### Move text to num problem

- **From:** Christopher Pomasl <pomasl-NOSpam@starband.net>
- **Date:** 2007-01-03T13:19:41-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<pan.2007.01.03.20.19.38.644379@starband.net>`

```
I am having a problem and it does not seem to make sense to me because I
didn't THINK there should be a difference.
 
Consider the following:
pp 5655-G53 IBM Enterprise COBOL for z/OS and OS/390 3.2.0

01  Buffer-Text.
  05  interim stuff
  05  Buffer-Text-48-52.
    10  Buffer-Text-40-50   Pic x(3).
    10  Filler              Pic x(2).
  05  trailing stuff of 80 byte "card"
 
01  Variables.
  05  stuff
  05  No-9  Pic 9(9).
  05  more stuff

If version = 115
  Move Buffer-Text-48-52 to No-9
  ...
Else
  Move Buffer-Text-48-50 to No-9
  ...
End-if.

Now the move with the elementary item works "correctly" and fills
in the zeros in the zoned numeric, but the move of the group item does not.

Test1 the incoming data is version 115 and 48-52 is 00004
and No-9 ends up with '00004    '
 
Test2 the incoming data is version 110 and 48-50 is 004 (48-52 is '004 L')
and No-9 ends up with '000000004'

It seems to me that group item -48-52 should be treated the same as PIC
x(5) and should populate No-9 correctly.  If this is the case I will
contact IBM and gripe.

Thanks, 
Chris
Senior Software Engineer, CA, inc
Always remember, you are unique...just like everyone else.
```

#### ↳ Re: Move text to num problem

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2007-01-03T20:31:05+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<doUmh.208590$jO5.196868@fe06.news.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<pan.2007.01.03.20.19.38.644379@starband.net>`

```
From,
   http://publibz.boulder.ibm.com/cgi-bin/bookmgr_OS390/BOOKS/IGY3LR31/6.2.24.6

" group move is treated as though it were an alphanumeric-to-alphanumeric 
elementary move ..."

while from
  http://publibz.boulder.ibm.com/cgi-bin/bookmgr_OS390/BOOKS/IGY3LR31/6.2.24.2

(for elementary moves)

"When the receiving field is:  ...
    Numeric or numeric-edited: ...

  When the category of the sending item is alphanumeric,  alphanumeric-edited, 
national, or national-edited, the data is moved  as if the sending item were 
described as an unsigned integer. "
```

## Thread: Fujitsu NetCOBOL for .NET v2.1 MessageBox question — 10 msgs

_Topics: [`Compilers and vendors`](topics.md#compilers)_

### Fujitsu NetCOBOL for .NET v2.1 MessageBox question

- **From:** wesley_3@hotmail.com
- **Date:** 2007-01-05T13:52:18-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1168033938.313462.16260@s80g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>`

```
Is there a way to set the button types on a MessageBox using Fujitsu
NetCOBOL for .NET v2.1?  I can invoke a messagebox and set the text and
caption, but can't seem to figure out how to set the button types.  I
want to be able to be able to display the Yes and No buttons rather
than the default of OK.
```

#### ↳ Re: Fujitsu NetCOBOL for .NET v2.1 MessageBox question

- **From:** "HeyBub" <heybubNOSPAM@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2007-01-05T19:18:38-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<12ptu7dodrkl26e@news.supernews.com>`
- **References:** `<1168033938.313462.16260@s80g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>`

```
wesley_3@hotmail.com wrote:
> Is there a way to set the button types on a MessageBox using Fujitsu
> NetCOBOL for .NET v2.1?  I can invoke a messagebox and set the text
> and caption, but can't seem to figure out how to set the button
> types.  I want to be able to be able to display the Yes and No
> buttons rather than the default of OK.

I'm speaking from PowerCOBOL 6 environment.

Look at the DisplayMessage Method. There you'll find oodles of possibilities 
for the buttons (and the icons displayed):

POW-DMDEFAULT is the OK button, but others are:

POW-DMOK (The default):
  A single OK button.
POW-DMOKCANCEL:
  OK and Cancel buttons.
POW-DMABORTRETRYIGNORE:
  Abort (Stop), Retry and Ignore buttons.
POW-DMYESNOCANCEL:
  Yes, No and Cancel buttons.
POW-DMYESNO:
  Yes and No buttons.
POW-DMRETRYCANCEL:
  Retry and Cancel buttons.

We never use them. Instead we wrote our own message box which gets passed a 
message number. The routine looks up the message in a message file, displays 
the message on the screen, and gets a response from the user. Each message 
has its own collection of possible answers. These answers range from a 
simple "OK" to "Yes, No, Ignore"

The routine also logs the message and the user response to an event file.
```

#### ↳ Re: Fujitsu NetCOBOL for .NET v2.1 MessageBox question

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2007-01-06T16:21:20+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<508ithF1cupruU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<1168033938.313462.16260@s80g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>`

```

<wesley_3@hotmail.com> wrote in message 
news:1168033938.313462.16260@s80g2000cwa.googlegroups.com...
> Is there a way to set the button types on a MessageBox using Fujitsu
> NetCOBOL for .NET v2.1?  I can invoke a messagebox and set the text and
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>

The third parameter should be a DotNet MessageBoxButtons constant....

try...

invoke objMyMsgBx "Show"
           using  by value "This is my text"
                                  "This is the caption"
                                  MessageBoxButtons.YesNo
end-invoke

Or something similar... I don't have NetCOBOL for DotNET so I can't give you 
the exact syntax...

In C# it is...

DialogResult  reponse = MessageBox.Show("this is text", "this is a caption", 
MessageBoxButtons.YesNo);

if (response = "Yes") ...

Both 'DialogResult' and 'MessageBoxButtons' are types. I'm not sure how 
DotNET COBOL will deal with these...

I would expect your environment to know about the MessageBoxButtons 
constants, but maybe it doesn't. Pass the ButtonType by value even if the 
other 2 are passed by reference.

You could try getting the response so you can see which button was 
pressed...

invoke objMyMsgBx "Show"
           using  ws-text
                     ws-caption
           by value
                      YesNo of  MessageBoxButtons    *> not sure of exact 
syntax here -  possible alternatives...
                                                                          *> 
YesNo
                                                                          *> 
<some Fujitsu reserved prefix> -YesNo
                                                                          *> 
36
                                                                             
   ...
           returning  response
end-invoke

If the 36 value works, response will be 6 if the user clicks Yes.  (I based 
this on some Windows Script Hosting I just tested, but it is using VB 
MsgBox, not Windows Forms. I believe DotNET uses Forms...)

As noted above, "response" is of type DialogResult which can't be defined in 
normal COBOL (maybe it can in DotNET?) I tried to get C# to cast it to 
string but it wouldn't... Probably the DotNET compiler deals with it; I 
can't experiment but you may have to. Depending on what the compiler does, 
you may not need the type ("MessageBoxButtons") in the parameter.
If all else fails, try Fujitsu support.

Please post the successful code here; I'll be very interested to see how it 
is resolved.

Pete.
```

>types.  I guess I'll have to figure something else out.

You have to declare them in the repository for your class.
repository.
    class Form as "System.Windows.Forms.Form"
    class DialogResult as "System.Windows.Forms.DialogResult"
    class MessageBoxButtons as "System.Windows.Forms.MessageBoxButtons"
    class MessageBox as "System.Windows.Forms.MessageBox"
    property YesNo
    property Yes
    .

I haven't worked with .NET enumerations in COBOL yet (DialogResult and
MessageBoxButtons are enumerations), but I think this should work.

Frank

Frank Swarbrick
Senior Developer/Analyst - Mainframe Applications
FirstBank Data Corporation - Lakewood, CO  USA

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Fujitsu NetCOBOL for .NET v2.1 MessageBox question

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** wesley_3@hotmail.com
- **Date:** 2007-01-08T12:25:35-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1168287934.994537.109040@38g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<1168033938.313462.16260@s80g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <508ithF1cupruU1@mid.individual.net> <1168268559.572913.120270@i15g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <50fhavF1fsp96U2@mid.individual.net>`

```
Frank Swarbrick wrote:
> >
> >
…[110 more quoted lines elided]…
> FirstBank Data Corporation - Lakewood, CO  USA

Frank, thanks for the input.  I finally got it to work.  I didn't have
the Enum statements set up correctly in the Repository.  The following
is what I ended up using.

REPOSITORY.
           CLASS CLASS-MESSAGEBOX AS "System.Windows.Forms.MessageBox"
           ENUM ENUM-MBBUTTON AS
"System.Windows.Forms.MessageBoxButtons"
           ENUM ENUM-DIALOGRESULT AS
"System.Windows.Forms.DialogResult"
           PROPERTY PROP-YESNO AS "YesNo"
           PROPERTY PROP-YES AS "Yes"
           .

WORKING-STORAGE SECTION.
       01  MB-YESNO OBJECT REFERENCE ENUM-MBBUTTON.
       01  RET-VAL OBJECT REFERENCE ENUM-DIALOGRESULT.
       01  DIALOGRESULTYES OBJECT REFERENCE ENUM-DIALOGRESULT.


PROCEDURE DIVISION.

           SET MB-YESNO TO PROP-YESNO OF ENUM-MBBUTTON.
           SET DIALOGRESULTYES TO PROP-YES OF ENUM-DIALOGRESULT.
           INVOKE CLASS-MESSAGEBOX "Show" USING "Text" "Caption"
MB-YESNO RETURNING RET-VAL.
           IF RET-VAL = DIALOGRESULTYES
                  *> User chose yes
           ELSE
                  *> User chose no
           END-IF.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Fujitsu NetCOBOL for .NET v2.1 MessageBox question

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** "Frank Swarbrick" <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com>
- **Date:** 2007-01-08T17:48:20-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<50g72mF1g1rqvU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<1168033938.313462.16260@s80g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <508ithF1cupruU1@mid.individual.net> <1168268559.572913.120270@i15g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <50fhavF1fsp96U2@mid.individual.net> <1168287934.994537.109040@38g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>`

```
<wesley_3@hotmail.com> 01/08/07 1:25 PM >>>
>Frank Swarbrick wrote:
>> >
…[54 more quoted lines elided]…
>           END-IF.

Sounds good.  I think with Micro Focus both enums and delegates are
considered to be classes, so they are specified with the class-specifier in
the repository.

For what its worth, I think you could eliminate MB-YESNO and DIALOGRESULTYES
and simply have:

WORKING-STORAGE SECTION.
       01  RET-VAL OBJECT REFERENCE ENUM-DIALOGRESULT.

PROCEDURE DIVISION.

           INVOKE CLASS-MESSAGEBOX "Show" USING "Text" "Caption" PROP-YESNO
OF ENUM-MBBUTTON
             RETURNING RET-VAL.
           IF RET-VAL = PROP-YES OF ENUM-DIALOGRESULT
                  *> User chose yes
           ELSE
                  *> User chose no
           END-IF.

Just a personal preference, but I prefer to have as few temporary fields as
possible.  (Which is why I would also use an inline invoke, here, instead of
INVOKE with RETURNING into a temporary field.)

PROCEDURE DIVISION.

           IF CLASS-MESSAGEBOX::"Show"( "Text" "Caption" PROP-YESNO OF
ENUM-MBBUTTON) 
               = PROP-YES OF ENUM-DIALOGRESULT
                  *> User chose yes
           ELSE
                  *> User chose no
           END-IF.

Perhaps I was corrupted by too much C programming in my wayward youth.  :-)

On a related matter, are Micro Focus Net Express and Fujitsu NetCOBOL for
.NET the only two Cobol compilers that work with .NET?
Just wondering.

Frank
